Phase I Variables (polity-based)[edit]

General variables[edit]

♠ RA ♣ Edward A L Turner ♥

♠ Expert ♣ ♥

♠ UTM zone ♣ 42-T ♥

♠ Original name ♣ Kidarite Kingdom ♥ Kidarite Kingdom.[1] The term "Kidarites" reflects the dynastic name, derived from King Kidara; the people were Chionites or Huns.[2]

♠ Alternative names ♣ Kidarites; Chionites; Kidarite Huns; Huna; Honk; Kushans; Ta Yeuh-chih; Lesser Yeuh-chih ♥ Kidarites, Chionites (Latin authors), 'Huns who are Kidarites' (Greek authors), Huna (Indian chronicles), Honk' and Kushans (Armenian literature), Ta Yueh-chih or Lesser Yueh-chih (Chinese annals).[3] The term "Kidarites" reflects the dynastic name, derived from King Kidara; the people were Chionites or Huns.[4] Junagadh inscription of c457 CE which refers to the reign of Skandagupta (455-467 CE) is referring to the Kidarites (or Hephthalites) by the name 'Mlecchas'.[5]


♠ Peak Date ♣ 420-440 CE ♥ Greatest territorial extent first half of 5th century.[6]

During the reign of king Kidara "the Kidarite kingdom occupied vast territories to the north and south of the Hindu Kush."[7]

Scholars believe that the information about king Kidara's reign in the Pei-shih "was based on the report of Tung Wan sent to the West in 437."[8]

"Kidara's rise to power, the founding of his state and the annexation of the territories to the south of the Hindu Kush ... should be dated to an earlier period ... some time between 390 and 430, but probably before 410."[9]

Further territories may have been taken in India in the mid-5th century when "a considerable portion of central and western Panjab was under Kidarite rule" during the reign of the Gupta king Kumaragupta I (413-455 CE).[10]

Indian inscriptians that refer to reign of Skandagupta (455-467 CE) mention Huna invaders.[11]

the Sassanians "laid waste territories subject to the Kidarites and took fortresses" during Yazdgird II's eastern campaigns and that by 449 CE they had the advantage. however, sometimes the Kidarites got the best of it and in 456 CE they refused to pay tribute.[12]

Capital captured by Sassanids 467 CE which forced Kidarites to retreat south of Hindu Kush to Gandhara.[13]


Temporal bounds[edit]

♠ Duration ♣ 388-477 CE ♥

Of 2,000 coins minted in Samarkand 1st-5th CE only 7 have the name of Kidara which suggests "Kidarite rule was short-lived."[14] The numismatics of these coins suggests that the Kidara ones cannot be earlier than mid-4th CE.[15]

Sassanian-type Kidarite coins suggest an early relationship with the Sassanids - perhaps official recognition for Sassanian suzerainty. This could be as early as c350 CE and as late as 388 CE.[16]

300 CE

"It has been suggested that they conquered K'ang-chu and Sogdiana in c. 300 but the literary sources have not yet been corroborated by the archaeological evidence."[17]

350 CE

c350 CE Ammianus Marcellinus (XVI, 9.4) reports that the "Chionites (i.e. the Kidarites) fought in Syria as allies of the Sasanian king, Shapur II (309-379), at the siege of Amida (the modern Diyarbekir)."[18] They were led into battle by a new king called Grumbrates who had an alliance with Shapur II (Ammianus Marcellinus XVII, 5.1).[19] What had happened to the old king? Was this when the alliance was first agreed?

390 CE

"Kidara's rise to power, the founding of his state and the annexation of the territories to the south of the Hindu Kush ... should be dated to an earlier period ... some time between 390 and 430, but probably before 410."[20]
for reign of king Kidara narrative sources suggest c420s CE but numismatists agree his rule began c390 CE.[21]

End

"It was probably not Skandagupta's victories but a new wave of nomadic invaders from the north ... Hephthalites ... that put an end of the Kidarite state in Gandhara and Panjab."[22]
lost Tokharistan to Hepththalites in 467 CE, "residual North Indian kingdom, perhaps in Swat, until 477."[23]

possible kinglist, unknown source

Kidara I, Kungas, Varhran I, Grumbat, Kidara II, Brahmi Buddhatala, Unknown, Varhran II, Goboziko, Salanavira, Vinayaditya, Kandik
Chinese pilgrim Sung Yun visited Gandhara in 520 and discovered Hephthalites were rulers


♠ Degree of centralization ♣ confederate state ♥

During the reign of king Kidara "the Kidarite kingdom occupied vast territories to the north and south of the Hindu Kush." "the principal city of the Kidarites south of the Hindu Kush was situated near present day Peshawar ... Fu-lou-sha ... which probably represents Purushapura ... Its ruler was Kidara's son".[24] Accordng to the Chinese chronicle, the Pei-shih (Annals of the Wei Dynasty)

Supra-cultural relations[edit]

♠ preceding (quasi)polity ♣ Sassanid Empire I ♥ Accordng to the Chinese chronicle, the Pei-shih (Annals of the Wei Dynasty) "The original nucleus of the Kidarite state was the territory of Tokharistan (now northern Afghanistan and southern Uzbekistan and Tajikstan), which was previously part of the Kushan Empire and subsequently of the Kushano-Sasanians."[26]
♠ relationship to preceding (quasi)polity ♣ elite migration; continuity ♥ "It has been suggested that they conquered K'ang-chu and Sogdiana in c. 300 but the literary sources have not yet been corroborated by the archaeological evidence."[27] The Kidarites might have been present (rulers?) in Tokharistan under the Kushans.
♠ succeeding (quasi)polity ♣ Hephthalite Empire ♥
♠ Supracultural entity ♣ Eurasian nomadic; Persian; Indian ♥
♠ scale of supra-cultural interaction ♣ 9,000,000 ♥ km squared. Total area of Eurasian nomadic, Persian and Indian cultural regions would be on the scale of 9 million km2 (core regions of these areas).

♠ Capital ♣ Balkh; Gandhara ♥ Accordng to the Chinese chronicle, the Pei-shih (Annals of the Wei Dynasty) the capital was Ying-chien-shih, which "was probably located at the ancient capital of Bactria, near Balkh."[28] Another Chinese source the Wei-shu mistakenly claimed that the capital was transferred to another city.[29] Capital captured by Sassanids 467 CE which forced Kidarites to retreat south of Hindu Kush to Gandhara.[30]

♠ Language ♣ Sogdian; Bactrian; Pahlavi; Brahmi ♥ "We do not know what language the Kidarites spoke".[31] Coinage had "inscriptions in Sogdian, Bactrian, Middle Persian and Brahmi."[32] "The Bactrian script and language were used for a long time after the Kushan age but only small fragments of Bactrian literary works have been discovered so far."[33] Administration was carried out at a regional level and probably in the local language by administrators recruited from the majority settled population. ♠ Linguistic family ♣ Indo-European ♥ Coinage had "inscriptions in Sogdian, Bactrian, Middle Persian and Brahmi."[34] "We do not know what language the Kidarites spoke"[35] but Bactrian is coded as the main language for the Hephthalites and one of the three main language for the Kushans. Bactrian is an Indo-European language. Administration was carried out at a regional level and probably in the local language by administrators recruited from the majority settled population.

♠ Religion Genus ♣ Zoroastrianism; Buddhism; Hinduism ♥ There was "a local variety of Zoroastrianism (Mazdaism) in Tokharistan, various expressions of Buddhism and Hinduism in the territory of Gandhara and also, probably, the official Sasanian doctrine."[36]

Map[edit]

General Description[edit]

The Kidarite state in Central Asia (~ 388-477 CE) may have lasted less than 100 years, but its earliest phase under the suzerainty of the Sassanid Empire is not well known.[37] "It has been suggested that they conquered K'ang-chu and Sogdiana in c. 300 but the literary sources have not yet been corroborated by the archaeological evidence."[38]

The most influential ruler of the Kidarites was perhaps king Kidara: narrative sources place him in the c420s CE but numismatists agree his rule began c390 CE.[39] The Chinese chronicle Peo-Shih (Annals of the Wei Dynasty) say Kidara held "vast territories to the north and south of the Hindu Kush" and his most imporant city was near Peshawar, probably Purushapura,[40] the late capital of the Kushan Empire.

Much like the Kushan Empire little is known about how exactly they ruled their territories. The Kidarites founded new cities (Panjikent and Kushaniya), Kushaniya being a royal foundation[41] that shows that the Kidarites attempted to draw some of their legitimacy from the preceding Kushan period. Zeimal (1996) concludes that "It seems likely that the administrative and government structure created by the Kushans was left largely intact under the Kidarites."[42]

Social Scale[edit]

♠ Polity territory ♣ 250,000: 400 CE; [850,000-900,000]: 450 CE ♥ in squared kilometers

Google area calcultor and a 400 CE map by Thomas Lessman.[44] Including in maximum extent calculation c.450 CE the region of the Chionites (which includes Samarkand), East Kushans (or at least as far as the area that reaches the Punjab) and the area labeled Kidarites. For 400 CE just the core region of Bactria (Tokharistan)[45].

King Kidara incorporated Gandhara into the kingdom, and "four other territories to the north of it."[46]

Further territories may have been taken in India in the mid-5th century when "a considerable portion of central and western Panjab was under Kidarite rule" during the reign of the Gupta king Kumaragupta I (413-455 CE).[47]

"total absence of Gupta coins in the western regions of India and in Pakistan" at beginning of Skandagupta's (455-467 CE) reign.[48]

After defeat north of the Hindu Kush by Peroz (Sassanids) in alliance with Hephthalites, Kidarites retreated to Gandhara in India, later to be overrun by the Hephthalites.[49]

♠ Polity Population ♣ [1,000,000-1,500,000] ♥ People.

Estimate for the population of Bactria c400 CE.

Bactria included part of modern Afghanistan and the region McEverdy and Jones (1979) called Russian Turkestan. In 400 CE McEvedy and Jones estimate 2.5 million and 2 million for those entire regions, respectively.[50] At this time in history Bactria would have been the core area of settled population in both these regions (with perhaps the exception of Khwarezm region in Russian Turkestan). However, core Bactria is only a very small part of northern Afghanistan. I would estimate 500,000 at most for the Afghan region and 1,000,000 for the region in Russian Turkestan, and express this as a range of 1-1.5 million.

♠ Population of the largest settlement ♣ ♥ Inhabitants.

Samarkand or Balkh 400 CE

Gandhara c450 CE

Captured Gandhara in India.[51]

♠ Largest communication distance ♣ [500-700] ♥ in kilometers.

Balkh to Ghandara 500-600 KM.

Balkh to modern Tashkent 600-700 KM. Is this the Paykent in this quote?: Kidarite rule "coincided with the building of new fortifications" (Samarkand, Paykent).[52]

Hierarchical Complexity[edit]

♠ Settlement hierarchy ♣ 4 ♥ levels.

1. Capital

Balkh, Gandhara.
2. Large town / new foundations
Kidarite rule "coincided with ... the foundation of new cities such as Panjikent and Kushaniya. (The name of the latter probably indicates a Kidarite royal foundation, as neither the Great Kushans nor the Kushano-Sasanians had exerted control over that region.)"[53]
3. Small town ?
4. Village ?

♠ Administrative levels ♣ [3-5] ♥ levels.

1. King

Sources do not tell us whether the new Hephthalite polity was a dynastic regime change or a 'clash of armies'.[54] "It is known, however, that the name Kidara was kept, although now as an honorific title (meaning 'honoured', 'hero', 'valiant'), long after the Kidarite state had ceased to exist, just as the original Kidara used to style himself on coins Kusana Sahi (king of Kushan) many years after the fall of the Empire of the Kushans."[55]
Kidarites were a group of nomadic origin.[56]
Chinese chronicle the Pei-shih claimed that the Kidarites "'move around following their herds of cattle' .... On the other hand, it is known that there were Kidarite capitals both in Gandhara and Tokharistan, and thus that they lived in towns."[57]
"more accurate to think of the Kidarite state not as a unified society but one with a clear distinction between the conquerers - the ruling group - and their subject peoples, the latter preserving their own traditions."[58]


_ Central Administration _

2. Top administrator
Kidarite rule "coincided with ... the foundation of new cities such as Panjikent and Kushaniya. (The name of the latter probably indicates a Kidarite royal foundation, as neither the Great Kushans nor the Kushano-Sasanians had exerted control over that region.)"[59]
"It is tempting to draw an analogy with the vast state of the Kushans. This is not only because the Kidarites claimed to be the successors ... ; a no less important factor is that the former nomadic invaders came into possession of vast territories inhabited by settled agricultural peoples with a culture and traditions dating back many centuries, just as had been the case with the Tokharians ... who created the Kushan Empire. It seems likely that the administrative and government structure created by the Kushans was left largely intact under the Kidarites."[60]
3.
4. Scribes
4. Manager of a Mint
The Kidarite coinage was not a separate monetary system but "an adaptation to the local issues in each area they conquered. In Sogdiana small silver coins were issued ... They followed the design of early Sogdian coins ... In Tokharistan gold inars were issued in the name of Kidara, following the gold coins of the Kushano-Sasanians ... The silver coins of Sasanian type can be attributed to Gandhara and the area around. ... In their Indian territories the Kidarites also issued gold coins based on the model of the Late Kushan dinars".[61]
Economy was advanced enough that copper coinage was minted in quantities that implied it was used as 'small change'. Copper coin design was also an adaptation to existing currency in each region.[62]
The Kidarite monetary system "created favourable conditions for maintaining the established traditions in local trades. ... flourishing international trade networks and wide trading links between various regions of the Kidarite state."[63]
5. Mint worker


_ Provincial government _

2.
Clan and tribal organizations traditional to nomadic peoples were likely "reflected in the administrative structure of the state".[64]

Hephthalites

Western sources suggests the Hephthalites were "a tribal group distinct from and apparently sometimes hostile to" the Kidarites.[65]
Many instances when the Hephthalites were allies with the Sassanians against the Kidarites. Hephthalites also sided with Hormizd faction in dispute for Sassanian kingship.[66]


♠ Religious levels ♣ [1-2] ♥ levels.

There was "a local variety of Zoroastrianism (Mazdaism) in Tokharistan, various expressions of Buddhism and Hinduism in the territory of Gandhara and also, probably, the official Sasanian doctrine."[67]

"It appears that the Kidarites' beliefs had not yet developed into a rigid religious system".[68]

"The Buddhist religious centre in Old Termez, destroyed probably in the 360s-370s by the Sasanians, already lay in ruins".[69]
also at time of Kidarites were abandoned buildings and caves of monasteries.[70]

♠ irrigation systems ♣ inferred present ♥ During the Kushan period irrigation canals were constructed on large scale: "As a result of the extensive development of irrigation networks, practically all the main provinces of Central Asia were brought under cultivation during this period and the establishment of the major crop-growing oases was completed."[81] At least some of the irrigation infrastructure would have been maintained into the Kidarite period.
♠ drinking water supply systems ♣ inferred absent ♥ During the Kushan period wells feature in literary descriptions of cities.[82]
♠ markets ♣ inferred present ♥ Kidarite rule "coincided with ... the foundation of new cities such as Panjikent and Kushaniya. (The name of the latter probably indicates a Kidarite royal foundation, as neither the Great Kushans nor the Kushano-Sasanians had exerted control over that region.)"[83] We could infer that the new cities were built with market infrastructure. Economy was advanced enough that copper coinage was minted in quantities that implied it was used as 'small change'.[84] The Kidarite monetary system "created favourable conditions for maintaining the established traditions in local trades. ... flourishing international trade networks and wide trading links between various regions of the Kidarite state."[85]
♠ food storage sites ♣ inferred present ♥ Kidarite rule "coincided with ... the foundation of new cities such as Panjikent and Kushaniya. "it is possible to speak only of works of art of the Kidarite period, created by craftsmen and artists in the countries conquered by them but following standards and traditions that had no direct connection with the conquerers."[88]

Buddhist shrines in Ghandara which probably were not associated with the Kidarites. On the other hand they evidently did not destroy them.[89]

Measurement System[edit]

♠ Length ♣ inferred present ♥ "the former nomadic invaders came into possession of vast territories inhabited by settled agricultural peoples with a culture and traditions dating back many centuries, just as had been the case with the Tokharians ... who created the Kushan Empire. It seems likely that the administrative and government structure created by the Kushans was left largely intact under the Kidarites."[95] The merchants who engaged in the vibrant trade system can be inferred to have used measurement systems, e.g. for measuring textiles and food stuffs. There were gold, silver, copper coins.[96] The economy was advanced enough that copper coinage was minted in quantities that implied it was used as 'small change'.[97] The Kidarite monetary system "created favourable conditions for maintaining the established traditions in local trades. ... flourishing international trade networks and wide trading links between various regions of the Kidarite state."[98]

There were gold, silver, copper coins.[101] The economy was advanced enough that copper coinage was minted in quantities that implied it was used as 'small change'.[102] The Kidarite monetary system "created favourable conditions for maintaining the established traditions in local trades. ... flourishing international trade networks and wide trading links between various regions of the Kidarite state."[103]

It seems likely that the administrative and government structure created by the Kushans was left largely intact under the Kidarites."[104] The merchants who engaged in the vibrant trade system can be inferred to have used measurement systems, e.g. for measuring textiles and food stuffs. ♠ Steel ♣ present ♥ At this time in Central Asia if high-quality steel was used it would have been imported. The following sources suggest later dates for fine steel. However we code present because the Kidarites occupied northern India (a location repeatedly associated with fine steel) which as early as 1st CE was exporting iron and steel as far as East Africa.[135] Reference for high quality of the steel (no beginning date provided): “In the context of this work, it is important to note that crucible steel of fine quality was made at Herat, in Bukhara and in northern India.”[136] Reference for high quality of the steel (this one dates from 900 CE): "Further east from Merv along the Silk Road is a region praised for its iron and steel production by Greek, Islamic, and Chinese writers. The Sogdian state of Ustrushana, a mountainous region east of Samarkand, and the Ferghana basin ... material related to the medieval iron and steel industry has been uncovered here. Most relevant ... is a workshop excavated at a city-site of the +9th-13th centuries in Feghana, at Eski Achsy, Uzbekistan. ..” Crucible fragments ”The excavators consider that the process used here was direct production of steel from ore, just as He Tangkun argues for the Luoyang crucibles. It is quite possible, however, that they were (also) used in co-fusion steel production as suggested by the Merv excavators."[137] Fine steel swords may have been produced at an earlier time than 900 CE with the technology coming from northern India or from this region via Persia: In Tibet c700 CE "steel swords were certainly available through trade with Sogdia and Fergana ... and many steel blades are known from Central Asia from the late first millennium until the arrival of Genghis Khan in the early thirteenth century."[138] "The Sogdian cities of Samarqand and Bukhara probably also manufactured iron and steel weapons that were exported to Tibet. We know that by the early eighth century, the Sogdians, having probably borrowed the technology from the Sasanians, were manufacturing mail armor and offered suits of the material as gifts to the Tang court in 718. ... The Sasasnians may themselves have developed knowledge of steelmaking from contacts with northern India."[139] "The principal centres for the manufacture of steel weapons in Central Asia were Khwarazm, Ferghana and northern India.”[140]

♠ Incendiaries ♣ absent ♥ 'Persians threw vessels filled with sulphur, asphalt and naphtha into the enemy bulwarks. The first mention of the common place use of naphtha as an incendiary device in the annals of Islam is in connection with a raid in India in 777. During the siege of Heraklia in 803, however, stone balls wrapped in linen and covered with burning naphtha were hurled by mangonels against the ramparts. The fire would cling to the stones and cause them to burst. From that time onwards, the caliphate employed corps of naphtha-throwers and the Muslim fleets were provided with it as well. In order to protect themselves, people covered flammable materials with newly flayed animal skins and those who handled the naphtha wore clothes saturated with talc. Naphtha-filled glass grenades appeared first in 934, unless in that year the weapons were actually incendiary javelins, but the use of fire grenades was firmly established a few years later. ... The latest historian of Greek fire, Maurice Mercier, admits that during the thirteenth century, people began to use grenades filled with mealed gunpowder, called 'Syrian jugs', which had a most explosive force. [144]

General Characteristics of Warfare[edit]

Types of Warfare[edit]

♠ Inter-polity wars ♣ present ♥ E.g. Gupta Empire: "It seems that the enemies who threatened the very existence of the Gupta state included not only their feudatories, the Pushyamitras, but also the Kidarites. In the Junagadh inscription, which dates from c. 457 and also refers to the reign of Skandagupta, it is obviously the Kidarites (or the Hephthalites; see pages 141–2) who are referred to under the name of the Mlecchas. Skandagupta’s victories over them were described as the conquest ‘of the whole world’. It is hard to establish what this claim implies in terms of geographical fact, but it appears that Skandagupta’s armies repulsed the Kidarite invasion somewhere on the River Sutlej (or perhaps further east). Thus, even after Skandagupta’s victories, central and western Panjab probably remained in the hands of the invaders, although he had managed to stop their advance eastwards."[150] E.g. also Sassanid Empire: ""During the second quarter of the fifth century the Kidarite state was again involved in serious fighting to the west with the Sasanian kings, who could not accept the loss of the territories they had conquered earlier from the Kushan Empire. Yazdgird II (438–457) took active measures to restore them to Sasanian rule and several eastern campaigns were undertaken during his reign. The struggle continued under Hormizd III (457–459) and also under Peroz (459–484)."[151]

♠ Enslavement ♣ inferred present ♥ Kidarites captured by the Sassanids were likely enslaved. "Slavery was more widespread in the Sasanid period. There were different categories of slave: bandag (servant); ansahrig (outlander); wardag (captive). The servant was usually in the household or a fire temple. The ansahrig usually worked on a farm and was non-Zoroastrian. A wardag worked on the land, in construction or other manual labor."[164]

This was "abandoned at the turn of the 5th century like many other settlements in the Hissar valley. ... the destruction and laying waste of the area is likely to have been connected with the Kidarite invasion."[165]

Ritual variables[edit]

♠ RA ♣ Dr Robert Harding ♥

Using the ritual sheet of the preceding Kushan Empire. - ET

"the former nomadic invaders came into possession of vast territories inhabited by settled agricultural peoples with a culture and traditions dating back many centuries, just as had been the case with the Tokharians ... who created the Kushan Empire. It seems likely that the administrative and government structure created by the Kushans was left largely intact under the Kidarites."[172]
During the Kidarite period there was "a local variety of Zoroastrianism (Mazdaism) in Tokharistan, various expressions of Buddhism and Hinduism in the territory of Gandhara and also, probably, the official Sasanian doctrine."[173]

♠ Expert ♣ Dr Julia Shaw ♥

"In spite of the scanty evidence, fragmentary in many respects, we can draw some general conclusions about religious life throughout the territory of the Kushans. It was highly developed and differentiated. The religious movements of India - Sivaism, Vishnuism, Jainism and Buddhism with their different schools - penetrated Central Asia, as did Indian merchants when Kushan rule facilitated long-distance international trade. In eastern Iran the Indian religions met the Greek divinities, Zoroastrianism and many local pre-Zoroastrian forms of worship, and encountered the ancient Iranian religious ideas of the northern Iranian equestrian nomads. The Kushan kings selected for themselves from this immense variety those religious elements, ideas and forms of cults which fitted their ancient religious traditions and strengthened the religious ideology of their royal power. So the ‘Kushan pantheon’ appearing on the coins represents only a selection of the religious cults of their empire.

"None the less, the Kushan kings were well aware of the current trends in religious life and followed them. The most important was syncretism. The great religions influenced one another and began slowly to absorb the local cults. In Bactria the syncretic cult of Siva achieved great success; and on the coins of Bazodeo (Vasudcva), the last Great Kushan king, Siva was the sole divinity used, a figure that apparently combined Greek, Iranian and non-Sivaite Indian elements." [174]

Largest scale collective ritual of the official cult[edit]

♠ Name ♣ Nowruz ♥ Little is known about the ritual practices and festivals of the Kushan Empire. However, it seems like a reasonable inference that Nowruz, the Zoroastrian New Year/Spring festival, would have been an important celebration, given its continuous popularity from (at least) Arsacid times up until the twentieth century [175]. That it would have been celebrated by non-Zoroastrians as well as Zoroastrians is suggested by two things: first, the fact that Zoroastrian orthodoxy never appears to have firmly established itself in this NGA, and, second, by the fact that the festival was celebrated by all in Islamic times [176], which suggests that something about it (most likely its euphoric nature, and the fact that it celebrated something as "natural" as the coming of Spring) facilitated inclusion. Unfortunately, there are no descriptions of how this festival would have been celebrated under Kushan rule; however, the earliest description of this festival dates to the immediately preceding polity, the Parthians, and it may therefore be used as a reasonable analogue for Kushan Nowruz--though, of course, an expert would have to confirm this. "The Arsacid period also provides the earliest description of Nowruz festivities. This comes from the romantic epic Vis u Ramin, which was identified by V. M. Minorsky (1946, 1947, 1954 and 1962, with all these articles collected and revised by him in 1964, pp. 151-99) as by origin a Parthian oral work, which has passed through an MP version and exists in the classical Persian rendering of Gorgāni (ed. M. A. Todua and A.A. Gwakharia. French tr. by H. Mass é. Eng. tr. by G. Morrison, in which the episodes are numbered as in the earlier editions by M. Minovi and M. J. Mahjub. For further bibliography see de Blois, 1992, pp. 165-67). The poem has plainly undergone revisions and extensions in the course of its long transmission, but much has been shown to belong to the Parthian original. This includes the royal banquet with which the poem begins. Not only is such a banquet a characteristic way to launch an epic, but what happens at this one is essential to the story. The host is Mobad, lord of Marv, that is, a vassal king of the Arsacids; but in the epic his concept often blurs, as here, with that of a Parthian Great King. So he is termed lord of the earth and greatest of all kings, and his guests are vassal kings and nobles, with their ladies, from all parts of Iran, including Pārs. The banquet is held in the open, under blossoming trees, and wine flows freely to the sound of minstrelsy and birdsong. Meantime the King’s humbler subjects are also celebrating out of doors, in field and garden, likewise with wine and music, some racing their horses, others dancing or picking flowers; and in the days that follow the King rides out, magnificently attended, and distributes largess lavishly. (Ed. Todua and Gwakharia, pp. 34-35. tr. Morrison, pp. 19-21.)." [177]
♠ Frequency for the ritual specialist ♣ yearly ♥ This festival celebrated the New Year and the arrival of Spring [178].
♠ Frequency per participant ♣ yearly ♥ This festival celebrated the New Year and the arrival of Spring [179].
♠ Frequency for the audience ♣ not applicable ♥ This festival celebrated the New Year and the arrival of Spring [180]. Thomas Cressy: As the ritual appears to have been celebrated by all, there seems no evidence of passive spectators. Coded as 'not applicable' instead of 'yearly'.
♠ Duration ♣ 24 ♥ 1 day 24 hours on basis ritual is the whole day There is some evidence, later in history, that this festival was extended, rather then comprising a single New Year's Day [181]. In the absence of similar evidence for this polity, though, it seems safer to assume the minimal duration of one day.

Most euphoric collective ritual of the official cult[edit]

♠ Name ♣ Nowruz ♥ Little is known about the ritual practices and festivals of the Kushan Empire. However, it seems like a reasonable inference that Nowruz, the Zoroastrian New Year/Spring festival, would have been an important celebration, given its continuous popularity from (at least) Arsacid times up until the twentieth century [210]. That it would have been celebrated by non-Zoroastrians as well as Zoroastrians is suggested by two things: first, the fact that Zoroastrian orthodoxy never appears to have firmly established itself in this NGA, and, second, by the fact that the festival was celebrated by all in Islamic times [211], which suggests that something about it (most likely its euphoric nature, and the fact that it celebrated something as "natural" as the coming of Spring) facilitated inclusion. Unfortunately, there are no descriptions of how this festival would have been celebrated under Kushan rule; however, the earliest description of this festival dates to the immediately preceding polity, the Parthians, and it may therefore be used as a reasonable analogue for Kushan Nowruz--though, of course, an expert would have to confirm this. "The Arsacid period also provides the earliest description of Nowruz festivities. This comes from the romantic epic Vis u Ramin, which was identified by V. M. Minorsky (1946, 1947, 1954 and 1962, with all these articles collected and revised by him in 1964, pp. 151-99) as by origin a Parthian oral work, which has passed through an MP version and exists in the classical Persian rendering of Gorgāni (ed. M. A. Todua and A.A. Gwakharia. French tr. by H. Mass é. Eng. tr. by G. Morrison, in which the episodes are numbered as in the earlier editions by M. Minovi and M. J. Mahjub. For further bibliography see de Blois, 1992, pp. 165-67). The poem has plainly undergone revisions and extensions in the course of its long transmission, but much has been shown to belong to the Parthian original. This includes the royal banquet with which the poem begins. Not only is such a banquet a characteristic way to launch an epic, but what happens at this one is essential to the story. The host is Mobad, lord of Marv, that is, a vassal king of the Arsacids; but in the epic his concept often blurs, as here, with that of a Parthian Great King. So he is termed lord of the earth and greatest of all kings, and his guests are vassal kings and nobles, with their ladies, from all parts of Iran, including Pārs. The banquet is held in the open, under blossoming trees, and wine flows freely to the sound of minstrelsy and birdsong. Meantime the King’s humbler subjects are also celebrating out of doors, in field and garden, likewise with wine and music, some racing their horses, others dancing or picking flowers; and in the days that follow the King rides out, magnificently attended, and distributes largess lavishly. (Ed. Todua and Gwakharia, pp. 34-35. tr. Morrison, pp. 19-21.)." [212]
♠ Frequency for the ritual specialist ♣ yearly ♥ This festival celebrated the New Year and the arrival of Spring [213].
♠ Frequency per participant ♣ yearly ♥ This festival celebrated the New Year and the arrival of Spring [214].
♠ Frequency for the audience ♣ not applicable ♥ This festival celebrated the New Year and the arrival of Spring [215]. Thomas Cressy: As the ritual appears to have been celebrated by all, there seems no evidence of passive spectators. Coded as 'not applicable' instead of 'yearly'.
♠ Duration ♣ 24 ♥ 1 day 24 hours on basis ritual is the whole day There is some evidence, later in history, that this festival was extended, rather then comprising a single New Year's Day [216]. In the absence of similar evidence for this polity, though, it seems safer to assume the minimal duration of one day.

Limits on Power of the Chief Executive[edit]

Legal (formal) limits[edit]

♠ Executive power is separate or independent from military ♣ absent ♥ Kidarites were a group of nomadic origin[245] however "there were Kidarite capitals both in Gandhara and Tokharistan, and thus ... they lived in towns."[246] Clan and tribal organizations traditional to nomadic peoples were likely "reflected in the administrative structure of the state and in the organization of the army".[247] It is "more accurate to think of the Kidarite state not as a unified society but one with a clear distinction between the conquerers - the ruling group - and their subject peoples, the latter preserving their own traditions."[248] The Kidarites imposed their rule over the settled people and their administrative infrastructure so the Kidarite king had full control over the military.
♠ Executive power is separate or independent from legislative ♣ inferred present ♥ Kidarites were a group of nomadic origin[249] however "there were Kidarite capitals both in Gandhara and Tokharistan, and thus ... they lived in towns."[250] Clan and tribal organizations traditional to nomadic peoples were likely "reflected in the administrative structure of the state and in the organization of the army".[251] It is "more accurate to think of the Kidarite state not as a unified society but one with a clear distinction between the conquerers - the ruling group - and their subject peoples, the latter preserving their own traditions."[252] There likely was no central administration as such, more likely regional administrations which the Kidarites may have supervised. This may be suggested by the Kidarite coinage which was not a separate monetary system but "an adaptation to the local issues in each area they conquered. In Sogdiana small silver coins were issued ... They followed the design of early Sogdian coins ... In Tokharistan gold dinars were issued in the name of Kidara, following the gold coins of the Kushano-Sasanians ... The silver coins of Sasanian type can be attributed to Gandhara and the area around. ... In their Indian territories the Kidarites also issued gold coins based on the model of the Late Kushan dinars".[253] The Kidarites imposed their rule over the settled people and their administrative infrastructure so the Kidarite king had full control over the military. Law-making and legislation, however, was probably largely left in the hands of the regional administrations and their own traditions which all displayed distinct characteristics through their coinage.
♠ Executive power is separate or independent from judiciary ♣ inferred present ♥ Kidarites were a group of nomadic origin[254] however "there were Kidarite capitals both in Gandhara and Tokharistan, and thus ... they lived in towns."[255] Clan and tribal organizations traditional to nomadic peoples were likely "reflected in the administrative structure of the state and in the organization of the army".[256] It is "more accurate to think of the Kidarite state not as a unified society but one with a clear distinction between the conquerers - the ruling group - and their subject peoples, the latter preserving their own traditions."[257] There likely was no central administration as such, more likely regional administrations which the Kidarites may have supervised. This may be suggested by the Kidarite coinage which was not a separate monetary system but "an adaptation to the local issues in each area they conquered. In Sogdiana small silver coins were issued ... They followed the design of early Sogdian coins ... In Tokharistan gold inars were issued in the name of Kidara, following the gold coins of the Kushano-Sasanians ... The silver coins of Sasanian type can be attributed to Gandhara and the area around. ... In their Indian territories the Kidarites also issued gold coins based on the model of the Late Kushan dinars".[258] The Kidarites imposed their rule over the settled people and their administrative infrastructure so the Kidarite king had full control over the military. Law-making and legislation, however, was probably largely left in the hands of the regional administrations and their own traditions which all displayed distinct characteristics through their coinage.

Legal (formal) freedoms[edit]

♠ Right to petition executive ♣ ♥ unknown
♠ Critique of executive by government ♣ present ♥ "It seems likely that the administrative and government structure created by the Kushans was left largely intact under the Kidarites."[259] There likely was no central administration as such, more likely regional administrations which the Kidarites may have supervised. This may be suggested by the Kidarite coinage which was not a separate monetary system but "an adaptation to the local issues in each area they conquered. In Sogdiana small silver coins were issued ... They followed the design of early Sogdian coins ... In Tokharistan gold inars were issued in the name of Kidara, following the gold coins of the Kushano-Sasanians ... The silver coins of Sasanian type can be attributed to Gandhara and the area around. ... In their Indian territories the Kidarites also issued gold coins based on the model of the Late Kushan dinars".[260] The Kidarites as a nomadic people with no experience of settled government were constrained by their lack of knowledge and dependence on the existing administrative structures and skills of the settled people.
♠ Critique of executive by non-government ♣ inferred present ♥ "It seems likely that the administrative and government structure created by the Kushans was left largely intact under the Kidarites."[261] There likely was no central administration as such, more likely regional administrations which the Kidarites may have supervised. This may be suggested by the Kidarite coinage which was not a separate monetary system but "an adaptation to the local issues in each area they conquered. In Sogdiana small silver coins were issued ... They followed the design of early Sogdian coins ... In Tokharistan gold inars were issued in the name of Kidara, following the gold coins of the Kushano-Sasanians ... The silver coins of Sasanian type can be attributed to Gandhara and the area around. ... In their Indian territories the Kidarites also issued gold coins based on the model of the Late Kushan dinars".[262] The Kidarites as a nomadic people with no experience of settled life so were constrained by their lack of knowledge and dependence on the existing skills of the settled people. One area where they may have taken advice from non-government is trade, i.e. from merchants, perhaps on the protection or maintenance of trade routes. The Kidarite monetary system "created favourable conditions for maintaining the established traditions in local trades. ... flourishing international trade networks and wide trading links between various regions of the Kidarite state."[263]

Power distributed[edit]

♠ Sanction of executive by government ♣ inferred absent ♥ It is "more accurate to think of the Kidarite state not as a unified society but one with a clear distinction between the conquerers - the ruling group - and their subject peoples, the latter preserving their own traditions."[264] The original Kidara used to style himself on coins Kusana Sahi (king of Kushan)".[265] "just as had been the case with the Tokharians ... who created the Kushan Empire. It seems likely that the administrative and government structure created by the Kushans was left largely intact under the Kidarites."[266] Very unlikely that the existing administrative apparatus was able to sanction the Kidarite king whose rule over them was imposed through force.
♠ Sanction of executive by non-government ♣ inferred absent ♥ It is "more accurate to think of the Kidarite state not as a unified society but one with a clear distinction between the conquerers - the ruling group - and their subject peoples, the latter preserving their own traditions."[267] The original Kidara used to style himself on coins Kusana Sahi (king of Kushan)".[268] "just as had been the case with the Tokharians ... who created the Kushan Empire. It seems likely that the administrative and government structure created by the Kushans was left largely intact under the Kidarites."[269] Very unlikely that the settled people were able to sanction the Kidarite king whose rule over them was imposed through force.
♠ Constraint on executive by government ♣ present ♥ "It seems likely that the administrative and government structure created by the Kushans was left largely intact under the Kidarites."[270] There likely was no central administration as such, more likely regional administrations which the Kidarites may have supervised. This may be suggested by the Kidarite coinage which was not a separate monetary system but "an adaptation to the local issues in each area they conquered. In Sogdiana small silver coins were issued ... They followed the design of early Sogdian coins ... In Tokharistan gold inars were issued in the name of Kidara, following the gold coins of the Kushano-Sasanians ... The silver coins of Sasanian type can be attributed to Gandhara and the area around. ... In their Indian territories the Kidarites also issued gold coins based on the model of the Late Kushan dinars".[271] The Kidarites as a nomadic people with no experience of settled government were constrained by their lack of knowledge and dependence on the existing administrative structures and skills of the settled people.
♠ Constraint on executive by non-government ♣ inferred present ♥ It is "more accurate to think of the Kidarite state not as a unified society but one with a clear distinction between the conquerers - the ruling group - and their subject peoples, the latter preserving their own traditions."[272] The original Kidara used to style himself on coins Kusana Sahi (king of Kushan)".[273] "just as had been the case with the Tokharians ... who created the Kushan Empire. It seems likely that the administrative and government structure created by the Kushans was left largely intact under the Kidarites."[274] The Kidarites as a nomadic people with no experience of settled life were constrained by their lack of knowledge and dependence on the existing settled cultures and the skills of their people. For example, if the king wanted his son to be educated to read and write in the local language then he would have to hire a teacher from a non-Kidarite family. Though they imposed themselves on the settled people through force they would have to make compromises if they wished to assimilate to settled life, which they apparently attempted to do as they had Kidarite capitals "both in Gandhara and Tokharistan, and thus ... they lived in towns."[275].
♠ Impeachment ♣ inferred absent ♥ It is "more accurate to think of the Kidarite state not as a unified society but one with a clear distinction between the conquerers - the ruling group - and their subject peoples, the latter preserving their own traditions."[276] The original Kidara used to style himself on coins Kusana Sahi (king of Kushan)".[277] "just as had been the case with the Tokharians ... who created the Kushan Empire. It seems likely that the administrative and government structure created by the Kushans was left largely intact under the Kidarites."[278] Very unlikely that the settled people were able to impeach the Kidarite king whose rule over them was imposed through force.

♠ Informal constraint on executive ♣ present ♥ Western sources suggests the Hephthalites were "a tribal group distinct from and apparently sometimes hostile to" the Kidarites.[286] Clan and tribal organizations traditional to nomadic peoples were likely "reflected in the administrative structure of the state and in the organization of the army".[287]
♠ Informal impeachment ♣ ♥ unknown It is "more accurate to think of the Kidarite state not as a unified society but one with a clear distinction between the conquerers - the ruling group - and their subject peoples, the latter preserving their own traditions."[288] The original Kidara used to style himself on coins Kusana Sahi (king of Kushan)".[289] "just as had been the case with the Tokharians ... who created the Kushan Empire. It seems likely that the administrative and government structure created by the Kushans was left largely intact under the Kidarites."[290] Very unlikely that the settled people were able to impeach the Kidarite king whose rule over them was imposed through force. However, we do not know the details of Kidarite kingship tradition. Could the king be ousted on the agreement of the leaders of other Kidarite tribes?

Limits on executive power imposed by religious agents[edit]

♠ Critique of executive by religious agents ♣ inferred present ♥ The Kidarites do not appear to have maintained a strong priestly class. "It appears that the Kidarites' beliefs had not yet developed into a rigid religious system".[291] There was "a local variety of Zoroastrianism (Mazdaism) in Tokharistan, various expressions of Buddhism and Hinduism in the territory of Gandhara and also, probably, the official Sasanian doctrine."[292] The abandoned buildings and caves of monasteries[293] and the ruins of the Buddhist religious centre in Old Termez, likely destroyed by the Sasanians in the 360s-370s CE, suggests the Kidarites did not fund religious authorities.[294] Nevertheless "It seems likely that the administrative and government structure created by the Kushans was left largely intact under the Kidarites."[295] If the existing infrastructure had included a level of religious influence, such as in the law, then this would likely have been retained. We can infer from the Zoroastrian in Tokharistan (probably of official Sasanid doctrine) that the influence likely would have extended to local law-making and possibly the judiciary (with priests as judges) as this was the case the Sasanid polity at this time.
♠ Sanction of executive by religious agents ♣ inferred absent ♥ The Kidarites do not appear to have maintained a strong priestly class. "It appears that the Kidarites' beliefs had not yet developed into a rigid religious system".[296] There was "a local variety of Zoroastrianism (Mazdaism) in Tokharistan, various expressions of Buddhism and Hinduism in the territory of Gandhara and also, probably, the official Sasanian doctrine."[297] The abandoned buildings and caves of monasteries[298] and the ruins of the Buddhist religious centre in Old Termez, likely destroyed by the Sasanians in the 360s-370s CE, suggests the Kidarites did not fund religious authorities.[299] Nevertheless "It seems likely that the administrative and government structure created by the Kushans was left largely intact under the Kidarites."[300] If the existing infrastructure had included a level of religious influence, such as in the law, then this would likely have been retained. We can infer from the Zoroastrian in Tokharistan (probably of official Sasanid doctrine) that the influence likely would have extended to local law-making and possibly the judiciary (with priests as judges) as this was the case the Sasanid polity at this time. However, the religious agents were not powerful enough to sanction the Kidarite king.

It is possible they were motivated by traditional belief systems characteristic of Steppe peoples which may have included supernatural agents.

Central Bureaucracy[edit]

♠ State-level administrators ♣ inferred present ♥

Kidarites were a group of nomadic origin[328] however "there were Kidarite capitals both in Gandhara and Tokharistan, and thus ... they lived in towns."[329] It is "more accurate to think of the Kidarite state not as a unified society but one with a clear distinction between the conquerers - the ruling group - and their subject peoples, the latter preserving their own traditions."[330] "[J]ust as had been the case with the Tokharians ... who created the Kushan Empire. It seems likely that the administrative and government structure created by the Kushans was left largely intact under the Kidarites."[331] Whatever administrative structure existed presided over an economy that was advanced enough that copper coinage was minted in quantities that implied it was used as 'small change'.[332] The Kidarites may not have got directly involved in the existing administrative government structures. They were nomadic warriors who probably represented a military aristocracy. The government administrations in Balkh and Ghandara were likely recruited from the existing settled population. Additionally, as the Kidarites would have had very little experience of settled living, the administrators would have mostly been left to their own devices to collect taxes and maintain infrastructure - so long as they did not threaten the Kidarite rule, there was probably little reason for direct Kidarite intervention in the administration. This general situation may have been adapted as the Kidarites gained more experience of running a state but we have very little detail and the polity lasted less than 100 years.

♠ Prestige of state-level bureaucracy ♣ lower ♥

It is "more accurate to think of the Kidarite state not as a unified society but one with a clear distinction between the conquerers - the ruling group - and their subject peoples, the latter preserving their own traditions."[333] "[J]ust as had been the case with the Tokharians ... who created the Kushan Empire. It seems likely that the administrative and government structure created by the Kushans was left largely intact under the Kidarites."[334]

Authority over state-level administrators

♠ Direct ♣ inferred absent ♥ Kidarites were a group of nomadic origin[335] however "there were Kidarite capitals both in Gandhara and Tokharistan, and thus ... they lived in towns."[336] It is "more accurate to think of the Kidarite state not as a unified society but one with a clear distinction between the conquerers - the ruling group - and their subject peoples, the latter preserving their own traditions."[337] "[J]ust as had been the case with the Tokharians ... who created the Kushan Empire. Since the king no doubt claimed the legal authority over the administrators we cannot code the relationship between king and bureaucrat as indirect. The code cannot be diffuse either because the administrators, whilst mostly left alone, had to align the administration's interests to Kidarite rule. The relationship between the executive and the administrators was therefore mixed.

Local-level officials (provincial, regional, civic administration)[edit]

♠ Number of regional administrative units ♣ ♥

♠ Local-level administrators ♣ inferred present ♥

Kidarites were a group of nomadic origin[372] however "there were Kidarite capitals both in Gandhara and Tokharistan, and thus ... they lived in towns."[373] It is "more accurate to think of the Kidarite state not as a unified society but one with a clear distinction between the conquerers - the ruling group - and their subject peoples, the latter preserving their own traditions."[374] "[J]ust as had been the case with the Tokharians ... who created the Kushan Empire. It seems likely that the administrative and government structure created by the Kushans was left largely intact under the Kidarites."[375] Whatever administrative structure existed presided over an economy that was advanced enough that copper coinage was minted in quantities that implied it was used as 'small change'.[376] Kidarite coinage suggests the presence of local administrations which have imparted their own distinct influences on their design: the monetary system was "an adaptation to the local issues in each area they conquered. In Sogdiana small silver coins were issued ... They followed the design of early Sogdian coins ... In Tokharistan gold dinars were issued in the name of Kidara, following the gold coins of the Kushano-Sasanians ... The silver coins of Sasanian type can be attributed to Gandhara and the area around. ... In their Indian territories the Kidarites also issued gold coins based on the model of the Late Kushan dinars".[377]


Authority over local-level administrators

♠ Direct ♣ inferred absent ♥ Kidarites were a group of nomadic origin[378] however "there were Kidarite capitals both in Gandhara and Tokharistan, and thus ... they lived in towns."[379] It is "more accurate to think of the Kidarite state not as a unified society but one with a clear distinction between the conquerers - the ruling group - and their subject peoples, the latter preserving their own traditions."[380] "[J]ust as had been the case with the Tokharians ... who created the Kushan Empire. ♠ Mixed ♣ inferred present ♥ Kidarites were a group of nomadic origin[393] however "there were Kidarite capitals both in Gandhara and Tokharistan, and thus ... they lived in towns."[394] It is "more accurate to think of the Kidarite state not as a unified society but one with a clear distinction between the conquerers - the ruling group - and their subject peoples, the latter preserving their own traditions."[395] "[J]ust as had been the case with the Tokharians ... who created the Kushan Empire. It seems likely that the administrative and government structure created by the Kushans was left largely intact under the Kidarites."[396] Whatever administrative structure existed presided over an economy that was advanced enough that copper coinage was minted in quantities that implied it was used as 'small change'.[397] Kidarite coinage suggests the presence of local administrations which have imparted their own distinct influences on their design: the monetary system was "an adaptation to the local issues in each area they conquered. In Sogdiana small silver coins were issued ... They followed the design of early Sogdian coins ... In Tokharistan gold dinars were issued in the name of Kidara, following the gold coins of the Kushano-Sasanians ... The silver coins of Sasanian type can be attributed to Gandhara and the area around. ... In their Indian territories the Kidarites also issued gold coins based on the model of the Late Kushan dinars".[398] The Kidarites may not have got directly involved in the existing administrative government structures. They were nomadic warriors who probably represented a military aristocracy. The local administrations were likely recruited from the existing settled population. Additionally, as the Kidarites would have had very little experience of settled living, the administrators would have mostly been left to their own devices to collect taxes and maintain infrastructure - so long as they did not threaten the Kidarite rule, there was probably little reason for direct Kidarite intervention in the administration. Kidarite coinage appears to provide direct evidence for the influence local rulers had, in this case to have their own currency.

Description of the Normative Ideology

There was "a local variety of Zoroastrianism (Mazdaism) in Tokharistan, various expressions of Buddhism and Hinduism in the territory of Gandhara and also, probably, the official Sasanian doctrine."[462]


Deification of Rulers[edit]

♠ Rulers are legitimated by gods ♣ inferred present ♥ "The key to understanding the ideology of the Kidarite rulers probably lies in their tendency to consider themselves the heirs of the Kushan kings (many expressions of which have been mentioned above). Indeed, this is how they were seen by the neighbouring peoples. It is for future investigations (especially in the field of archaeology) to show how profoundly and consistently the Kushan heritage was assimilated by the Kidarites." [463] This is what we know about the relationship between Kushan rulers and their gods: "The Kushan kings derived their royal power from divine patrons, and so they were charismatic kings, human incarnations of divine might and power. As a consequence of their charisma, they also became objects of divine worship in dynastic sanctuaries." [464]

♠ Rulers control supernatural energy ♣ suspected unknown ♥ "The key to understanding the ideology of the Kidarite rulers probably lies in their tendency to consider themselves the heirs of the Kushan kings (many expressions of which have been mentioned above). Indeed, this is how they were seen by the neighbouring peoples. It is for future investigations (especially in the field of archaeology) to show how profoundly and consistently the Kushan heritage was assimilated by the Kidarites." [465] The following quote may be interpreted to mean that Kushan rulers were believed to hold supernatural powers, but it is not very clear: "The Kushan kings derived their royal power from divine patrons, and so they were charismatic kings, human incarnations of divine might and power. As a consequence of their charisma, they also became objects of divine worship in dynastic sanctuaries." [466]

♠ Rulers are descendants of gods ♣ {absent; present} ♥ "The key to understanding the ideology of the Kidarite rulers probably lies in their tendency to consider themselves the heirs of the Kushan kings (many expressions of which have been mentioned above). Indeed, this is how they were seen by the neighbouring peoples. It is for future investigations (especially in the field of archaeology) to show how profoundly and consistently the Kushan heritage was assimilated by the Kidarites." [467] There seems to be some disagreement regarding whether or not Kushan rulers were thought to descend from gods. "The Kushan kings derived their royal power from divine patrons, and so they were charismatic kings, human incarnations of divine might and power. As a consequence of their charisma, they also became objects of divine worship in dynastic sanctuaries." [468] "The divinity of kingship seems to have been the most conspicuous element in the Kushan political system. Their kings were not only accorded the title of 'devaputra' (Son of God), corresponding to the Chinese imperial title "t'ien-tzu" (Son of Heaven), but were deified after death and their statues were set up in a devakula (god house)." [469]

Normative Ideological Aspects of Equity and Prosociality[edit]

♠ Ideological reinforcement of equality ♣ [present; absent] ♥ There was "a local variety of Zoroastrianism (Mazdaism) in Tokharistan, various expressions of Buddhism and Hinduism in the territory of Gandhara and also, probably, the official Sasanian doctrine."[476] Zoroastrian eschatology held that the potential for resurrection and salvation was universal [477]. However, in the fifth century, a new sect, Mazdakism, advocated "egalitarianism in terms of sharing wealth and property, including women" and briefly resulted in "a land redistribution that diminished the power of both the priestly and upper classes, and benefitted the lower classes in both Iran and the client states to the west" [478]. This, as well as the emphasis placed in both iconography and inscriptions on the exhalted status of rulers and priests [479], suggests that the Zoroastrianism practiced by the Sasanians drew a stark line between rulers and commoners. Hinduism, of course, is usually associated with the existence of a caste system [480]. As for Buddhism, it is fundamentally egalitarian [481].

♠ Ideological thought equates rulers and commoners ♣ inferred absent ♥ There was "a local variety of Zoroastrianism (Mazdaism) in Tokharistan, various expressions of Buddhism and Hinduism in the territory of Gandhara and also, probably, the official Sasanian doctrine."[482] Zoroastrian eschatology held that the potential for resurrection and salvation was universal [483]. However, in the fifth century, a new sect, Mazdakism, advocated "egalitarianism in terms of sharing wealth and property, including women" and briefly resulted in "a land redistribution that diminished the power of both the priestly and upper classes, and benefitted the lower classes in both Iran and the client states to the west" [484]. This, as well as the emphasis placed in both iconography and inscriptions on the exhalted status of rulers and priests [485], suggests that the Zoroastrianism practiced by the Sasanians drew a stark line between rulers and commoners. Hinduism, of course, is usually associated with the existence of a caste system [486].
♠ Ideological thought equates elites and commoners ♣ inferred absent ♥ There was "a local variety of Zoroastrianism (Mazdaism) in Tokharistan, various expressions of Buddhism and Hinduism in the territory of Gandhara and also, probably, the official Sasanian doctrine."[487] Hinduism and Zoroastrianism both make a clear distinction between elites and commoners. Zoroastrians believed that elites were cosmologically distinct from commoners ('The omniscient Mazdean religion is likened to a mighty tree with one trunk (the mean), two main boughs (action and abstention), three branches (good thoughts, good words, and good deeds), four small branches (the estates of the priests, warriors, husbandmen, and artisans), five roots (the lord of the house, the village headman, the tribal chieftain, the ruler, and the highest religious authority, the representative of Zoroaster on earth ...), and above them all the head of all heads ... the king of kings, the ruler of the whole world.'[488]).
♠ Ideological thought equates all ethnicities ♣ suspected unknown ♥
♠ Ideological thought equates men and women ♣ {present; absent} ♥ There was "a local variety of Zoroastrianism (Mazdaism) in Tokharistan, various expressions of Buddhism and Hinduism in the territory of Gandhara and also, probably, the official Sasanian doctrine."[489] There is much disagreement regarding the Zoroastrian [490][491][492] and Buddhist [493] views of the relationships between the sexes.
♠ Ideological thought equates people of all locations ♣ suspected unknown ♥
♠ Ideological thought distinguishes rulers and commoners ♣ inferred present ♥ There was "a local variety of Zoroastrianism (Mazdaism) in Tokharistan, various expressions of Buddhism and Hinduism in the territory of Gandhara and also, probably, the official Sasanian doctrine."[494] Zoroastrian eschatology held that the potential for resurrection and salvation was universal [495]. However, in the fifth century, a new sect, Mazdakism, advocated "egalitarianism in terms of sharing wealth and property, including women" and briefly resulted in "a land redistribution that diminished the power of both the priestly and upper classes, and benefitted the lower classes in both Iran and the client states to the west" [496]. This, as well as the emphasis placed in both iconography and inscriptions on the exhalted status of rulers and priests [497], suggests that the Zoroastrianism practiced by the Sasanians drew a stark line between rulers and commoners. Hinduism, of course, is usually associated with the existence of a caste system [498].
♠ Ideological thought distinguishes elites and commoners ♣ inferred present ♥ There was "a local variety of Zoroastrianism (Mazdaism) in Tokharistan, various expressions of Buddhism and Hinduism in the territory of Gandhara and also, probably, the official Sasanian doctrine."[499] Hinduism and Zoroastrianism both make a clear distinction between elites and commoners. Zoroastrians believed that elites were cosmologically distinct from commoners ('The omniscient Mazdean religion is likened to a mighty tree with one trunk (the mean), two main boughs (action and abstention), three branches (good thoughts, good words, and good deeds), four small branches (the estates of the priests, warriors, husbandmen, and artisans), five roots (the lord of the house, the village headman, the tribal chieftain, the ruler, and the highest religious authority, the representative of Zoroaster on earth ...), and above them all the head of all heads ... the king of kings, the ruler of the whole world.'[500]).

♠ Ideological thought distinguishes people from different locations ♣ suspected unknown ♥

♠ Ideological reinforcement of inequality ♣ [present; absent] ♥ There was "a local variety of Zoroastrianism (Mazdaism) in Tokharistan, various expressions of Buddhism and Hinduism in the territory of Gandhara and also, probably, the official Sasanian doctrine."[505] Zoroastrian eschatology held that the potential for resurrection and salvation was universal [506]. However, in the fifth century, a new sect, Mazdakism, advocated "egalitarianism in terms of sharing wealth and property, including women" and briefly resulted in "a land redistribution that diminished the power of both the priestly and upper classes, and benefitted the lower classes in both Iran and the client states to the west" [507]. This, as well as the emphasis placed in both iconography and inscriptions on the exhalted status of rulers and priests [508], suggests that the Zoroastrianism practiced by the Sasanians drew a stark line between rulers and commoners. Hinduism, of course, is usually associated with the existence of a caste system [509]. As for Buddhism, it is fundamentally egalitarian [510].

♠ Ideological reinforcement of monogamy ♣ [absent; present] ♥ There was "a local variety of Zoroastrianism (Mazdaism) in Tokharistan, various expressions of Buddhism and Hinduism in the territory of Gandhara and also, probably, the official Sasanian doctrine."[511] Hinduism, Buddhism and Zoroastrianism all permitted polygamy [512][513][514][515].

♠ Ideology reinforces prosociality ♣ inferred present ♥ There was "a local variety of Zoroastrianism (Mazdaism) in Tokharistan, various expressions of Buddhism and Hinduism in the territory of Gandhara and also, probably, the official Sasanian doctrine."[516] That prosociality was an important moral value in the Kidarite kingdom may be inferred, perhaps, from the fact that it is promoted by all four of the above-mentioned world religions. Buddhism: “The twofold benefit of living a morally good life is linked to a twofold motivation: ‘Protecting oneself, one protects others; protecting others, one protects oneself ’ - just as each acrobat in a balancing act protects his partner by concentrating on himself, and protects himself by concentrating on his partner (see SN 47:19). If we take care of our own spiritual development, we render a service to others; and if we develop love towards others, we thereby also help ourselves. Accordingly, it is explicitly stated, someone who pursues the path of salvation only for his or her own benefit is to be censured, while the one who follows the path for one’s own benefit and for the benefit of others is to be commended (see AN 7:64).” [517] Hinduism: “According to Hindu Dharmashastras, of the four stages of life, the person who is at the householder stage is the anchor of society. The householder’s business is the maintenance and support of those in the other three stages of life: the students who have not yet entered into the world of work, the retirees who have seen the birth of grand- children and spotted their first gray hairs, and the renunciants, those who have left behind not only the world of work and material possession but who have also left behind family and self-interest for a life focused on spiritual freedom. Householders are the ones with material wealth, and their responsibility, their dharma, is to share it with others. [...] The link between the world of the householder and the world of the renunciant is gift giving—dana. The classic image is of the renouncer with the begging bowl, making the rounds for alms. On a daily basis, in Hindu society, food is given to sannyasins and sadhus, the world renouncers who have “cast off” from the settled world of “this shore.” ” [518] Zoroastrianism: Cantera says that 'from its very beginnings Zoroastrianism has developed an ethical imperative of assistance to the needy members of the community'.[519]

♠ charity ♣ inferred present ♥ There was "a local variety of Zoroastrianism (Mazdaism) in Tokharistan, various expressions of Buddhism and Hinduism in the territory of Gandhara and also, probably, the official Sasanian doctrine."[520] That charity was an important moral value in the Kidarite kingdom may be inferred, perhaps, from the fact that it is promoted by all four of the above-mentioned world religions. Buddhism: “Living a morally good life thus always brings a twofold benefit: Others are aided and one’s own spiritual development or karmic disposition is positively affected. This point is captured in one of the central Buddhist virtues: dana, which is ‘giving’ or ‘generosity’. ‘Giving’ creates a win-win situation. Not only is the gift’s recipient benefited but also the gift’s donor - particularly from a Buddhist point of view, since through giving one counters attachment in its deep-seated form of greedy grasping, of not letting-go.” [521] Hinduism: “According to Hindu Dharmashastras, of the four stages of life, the person who is at the householder stage is the anchor of society. The householder’s business is the maintenance and support of those in the other three stages of life: the students who have not yet entered into the world of work, the retirees who have seen the birth of grand- children and spotted their first gray hairs, and the renunciants, those who have left behind not only the world of work and material possession but who have also left behind family and self-interest for a life focused on spiritual freedom. Householders are the ones with material wealth, and their responsibility, their dharma, is to share it with others. [...] The link between the world of the householder and the world of the renunciant is gift giving—dana. The classic image is of the renouncer with the begging bowl, making the rounds for alms. On a daily basis, in Hindu society, food is given to sannyasins and sadhus, the world renouncers who have “cast off” from the settled world of “this shore.” ” [522] Zoroastrianism: Cantera says that 'from its very beginnings Zoroastrianism has developed an ethical imperative of assistance to the needy members of the community'.[523]
♠ production of public goods ♣ suspected unknown ♥
♠ communal dining ♣ inferred present ♥ Buddhist panal art depicts feasting, although they also might depict scenes from a drama.[524] "In Buddhist monasteries at Shaikhan Dheri, a site in the Gandharan region, archeologists have found many wine storage jars and distillation equipment. It is a puzzling find, suggesting that Buddhist monasteries were producing liquor, even though such activities were contrary to their centuries-old monastic discipline."[525]
♠ morality ♣ inferred present ♥ There was "a local variety of Zoroastrianism (Mazdaism) in Tokharistan, various expressions of Buddhism and Hinduism in the territory of Gandhara and also, probably, the official Sasanian doctrine."[526] but Buddhism, Hinduism, and Zoroastrianism all contain claims regarding proper moral behaviour. Buddhism: “If ‘thirst’ and ‘attachment’ constitute the one root of suffering - both the suffering one undergoes oneself in sansaric existence, and the suffering one inflicts upon others - it follows that any successful attempt to reduce one’s ‘thirst’ and to moderate one’s ‘attachment’ will also result in a life causing less suffering (at least less deliberately-inflicted suffering) to others - and this is what morality basically means for Buddhism: reducing suffering, alleviating suffering, causing less suffering. Morality is an intrinsic part of the path to salvation because it is part and parcel of the salvific process of overcoming ‘thirst’ and ‘attachment’.” [527] Hinduism: “Universal dharma relates to all humanity and it includes ethical virtues such as compassion, generosity, gratitude, and non-violence. These virtues that are said to be common to all humans are known as sadhārana (common) or sanatana (eternal) dharma. For example, the Law of Manu (X:63) argues that non-injury, truth, not stealing, purity and control of the senses summarise dharma for the four castes. Elsewhere Manu (VI:91- 2) asserts that avoiding backbiting, hypocrisy, greed, infatuation, anger and discontent is approved dharma for all the stages of life. The problem is that there are different views as to what the actuality of universal dharma is. Radhakrishnan, for example, argues for a sanatana dharma, based upon Advaita Vedanta thought, that is said to be present in all religious traditions. It is a kind of perennial philosophy that is at the heart of every religious tradition not just at the heart of the Hindu tradition. Others concentrate upon a universal dharma within the Hindu tradition itself but there is no absolute consensus as to what it is. Nevertheless, in theory, the ethical virtues of compassion, generosity, gratitude, and non-violence are universally regarded by most Hindus.” [528] Zoroastrianism: for a Zoroastrian worshipper, "The moral requirements of his faith make him self-reliant, bearing the responsibility for his own conduct and ultimate fate". [529]

Normative Ideological Aspects of Slavery[edit]

♠ Ideology forbids slavery for co-ethnics ♣ inferred absent ♥ There was "a local variety of Zoroastrianism (Mazdaism) in Tokharistan, various expressions of Buddhism and Hinduism in the territory of Gandhara and also, probably, the official Sasanian doctrine."[530] Buddhism [531], Hinduism [532] did not condemned slavery at the time. As for Zoroastrianism, it is difficult to find direct evidence of Achaemenid-period Zoroastrian attitudes to slavery, but 'there can be no doubt that the various departments of the Achaemenid court included a large number of slaves'.[533], and 'Under the Achaemenids in Babylonia and other conquered countries Persian nobles became large slave owners'.[534] If there was religious doctrine forbidding ownership of slaves, it was evidently not observed by the Achaemenid Persian elite. In later Sasanian law, founded in Zoroastrian principles, we find a distinction between freeborn men and slaves, which is inconsistent with a doctrinal rejection of the institution of slavery.[535] Finally, it is worth noting that slave labour was an important component of the Kidarite economy [536].
♠ Ideology forbids slavery for co-religionists ♣ inferred absent ♥ There was "a local variety of Zoroastrianism (Mazdaism) in Tokharistan, various expressions of Buddhism and Hinduism in the territory of Gandhara and also, probably, the official Sasanian doctrine."[537] Buddhism [538], Hinduism [539] did not condemned slavery at the time. As for Zoroastrianism, it is difficult to find direct evidence of Achaemenid-period Zoroastrian attitudes to slavery, but 'there can be no doubt that the various departments of the Achaemenid court included a large number of slaves'.[540], and 'Under the Achaemenids in Babylonia and other conquered countries Persian nobles became large slave owners'.[541] If there was religious doctrine forbidding ownership of slaves, it was evidently not observed by the Achaemenid Persian elite. In later Sasanian law, founded in Zoroastrian principles, we find a distinction between freeborn men and slaves, which is inconsistent with a doctrinal rejection of the institution of slavery.[542] Finally, it is worth noting that slave labour was an important component of the Kidarite economy [543].
♠ Ideology forbids slavery universally ♣ inferred absent ♥ There was "a local variety of Zoroastrianism (Mazdaism) in Tokharistan, various expressions of Buddhism and Hinduism in the territory of Gandhara and also, probably, the official Sasanian doctrine."[544] Buddhism [545], Hinduism [546] did not condemned slavery at the time. As for Zoroastrianism, it is difficult to find direct evidence of Achaemenid-period Zoroastrian attitudes to slavery, but 'there can be no doubt that the various departments of the Achaemenid court included a large number of slaves'.[547], and 'Under the Achaemenids in Babylonia and other conquered countries Persian nobles became large slave owners'.[548] If there was religious doctrine forbidding ownership of slaves, it was evidently not observed by the Achaemenid Persian elite. In later Sasanian law, founded in Zoroastrian principles, we find a distinction between freeborn men and slaves, which is inconsistent with a doctrinal rejection of the institution of slavery.[549] Finally, it is worth noting that slave labour was an important component of the Kidarite economy [550].

Normative Ideological Aspects of Human Sacrifice[edit]

♠ Human sacrifice ♣ [0-1] ♥ There was "a local variety of Zoroastrianism (Mazdaism) in Tokharistan, various expressions of Buddhism and Hinduism in the territory of Gandhara and also, probably, the official Sasanian doctrine."[551] That human sacrifice was not practiced in the Kidarite kingdom may perhaps be inferred from the fact it was not commonly practiced in either Hinduism, Zoroastrianism, or Buddhism. Buddhism categorically condemns the act of killing [552]. As for Zoroastrianism, there appears to be only one reference to human sacrifice among the Achaemenids (the best-known Zoroastrian polity among those preceding the Kidarite kingdom), and it is an unreliable one [553]. And as for Hinduism, human sacrifice seems to be mostly associated with Vedic times and with nineteenth-century Goddess worship [554].
